Vomiting That Fills the Mouth Nullifies Wudu
Ruling with proof on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomiting as a nullifier of wudu, identify the condition that the vomit must fill the mouth, and explain the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Vomiting nullifies wudu only if it fills the mouth — less does not break wudu.

This lesson clarifies an important ruling in Taharah: when vomiting breaks wudu. The Hanafi school sets a clear condition — the vomit must fill the mouth. Understanding this condition prevents both unnecessary re-wudu and unintentional invalidation of prayer. We will examine the textual proof from the hadith and the reasoning behind the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is vomiting of food, water, congealed blood, or bile. The ruling is that it nullifies wudu (ينقض الوضوء).
The ruling covers all four types of vomit listed.
2
Note the condition for nullification
The vomit must fill the mouth (إذا ملأ الفم). If it is less than a mouthful, wudu is not nullified according to the Hanafi school.
The condition is explicit in the matn.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith commands wudu after vomiting: 'Whoever is afflicted with vomiting... let him perform wudu.' The command indicates obligation, thus vomiting nullifies wudu.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted by the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the wisdom (hikma)
Vomiting is considered impure because it comes from the stomach and contacts impurities. The condition of filling the mouth ensures the impurity is significant.
This explains why only a mouthful nullifies.
5
Distinguish from other schools
Some schools like the Shafi'i do not consider vomiting a nullifier at all. The Hanafi school follows the hadith and the practice of the Companions.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) ينقض الوضوء (قيء طعام، أو ماء، أو علـق) ، وهـو : مـا اشتدت حمرته وجمد وهي سوداء محترقة. ( أوْ مِرَّةٍ) أي: صفراء (إذا مَلاً الفم)، لأنه يكون متنجساً بما في قعر المعدة، بفتح الميم وإسكان العين، فينتقض لقوله : « مَنْ أَصَابَهُ قَيْءٌ أَوْ رُعَافُ أَوْ قَلَسٌ أوْ مَدْي فَلْيَنْصَرف، فَلْيَتَوَضًا، ثُمَّ لِبَيْن عَلَى صَلاتِهِ وَهُوَ فِي ذَلِكَ لا يَتَكَلَّمُ ، وهو مذهب العشرة المبشرين بالجنة ، ومن تابعهم. ولأنه قاءَ فتوضأ » . قال الترمذي: وهو أصح شيء في الباب. ورواه الحاكم في ( المستدرك ) وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين ولم يخرجاه ولقوله : « يُعاد
And wudu is nullified by the vomiting of food, or water, or 'alaq (congealed blood that is intensely red and coagulated, black and burnt), or mirra (bile, i.e., yellow) if it fills the mouth. Because it becomes impure by what is in the bottom of the stomach (with fatḥa on the mīm and sukūn on the 'ayn). So it nullifies because of his statement: 'Whoever is afflicted with vomiting, or nosebleed, or qalas, or madhy, let him leave and perform wudu, then let him continue his prayer without speaking.' And this is the madhhab of the ten companions and those who followed them. And because he vomited and performed wudu. Al-Tirmidhi said: It is the most authentic thing on the topic. Al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak and said it is authentic according to the condition of the two shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim), but they did not record it. And because of his statement: 'He should repeat...'
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage establishes that vomiting that fills the mouth nullifies wudu. The proof is a hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) commands wudu after vomiting. The types of vomit include food, water, congealed blood (defined as black burnt blood), and bile. The condition is that the vomit must fill the mouth. The reason given is that the vomit is impure because it comes into contact with impurities in the stomach. The passage also mentions that this is the madhhab of the ten companions and many tabi'un. Al-Tirmidhi declared it the most authentic on the topic, and al-Hakim authenticated it according to the criteria of Bukhari and Muslim. The text ends with a fragment of another narration: 'He should repeat...' likely referring to repeating prayer if it occurred.

Key Terms
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the act of expelling stomach contents through the mouth
علق ('alaq): Congealed blood that is intensely red and coagulated, appearing black and burnt
مرة (mirrah): Bile; yellow digestive fluid
ملأ الفم (mala'a al-fam): Filled the mouth; condition that the vomit occupies the entire mouth cavity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Taharah — Purification

Ruling on Blood, Pus, and Vomit Nullifying Wudu
States ruling with proof and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which blood, pus, and vomit nullify wudu, explain the definition of 'mil' al-fam', and apply the ruling to various scenarios.

Core Idea
Blood, pus, vomit, and bile nullify wudu only if they fill the mouth — the mouth's dual nature requires this higher threshold.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the Hanafi rule for when exiting substances like blood and vomit break wudu. Unlike other body parts where any flow suffices, the mouth has a unique condition: the substance must fill the mouth (mil' al-fam). This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proofs, and the rationale behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The passage lists substances that nullify wudu: blood, pus, vomit (food, water, clot, bile). The hukm is that wudu becomes wajib upon their occurrence.
The nullifiers themselves are described; the consequence is the obligation to renew wudu.
2
Locate the dalil
What proofs are cited? The passage mentions the hadith: 'Whoever is afflicted by vomit or nosebleed... let him perform wudu.' Also the hadith of 'seven things that necessitate wudu' from al-Hakim. These are ahad hadiths.
The author notes the hadith is authentic according to Tirmidhi and al-Hakim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How do these proofs establish nullification? The imperative command 'falyatawadda' indicates obligation. The enumeration of seven items includes blood and vomit, showing they nullify.
The dalal is zanni because the chain is ahad, but the Hanafi school accepts it for obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on definition of mil' al-fam
What are the different opinions on what constitutes 'filling the mouth'? The passage lists several: difficulty closing mouth, inability to speak, exceeding half the mouth, etc. The relied-upon view (al-asahh) is that of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: 'what the mouth cannot close over except with difficulty'.
This tarjih is from al-Shurunbulali's commentary, supporting the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply to scenarios
How does the ruling apply to cases like blood from a wound or vomit? For the mouth, only mil' al-fam nullifies; for other body parts, flow (sayalan) is the condition. Examples: blood from eye flowing to the other side does not nullify because washing not recommended; a tick sucking blood if small does not nullify.
The distinction is based on whether the substance exits to an area that requires purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كدم، وقيح، وقيء طعام، أو ماءٍ، أَوْ عَلَقٍ، أَوْ مِرَّةٍ، إذا مَلاً الفم . وابن مسعود، وابن عباس وزيد بن ثابت، وأبي موسى الأشعري، وغيرهم من كبار الصحابة، وصدور التابعين، كالحسن البصري، وابن سيرين . والسيلان في غير السبيلين يتجاوز النجاسة إلى موضع يلحقه حكم التطهير، أي: يطلب تطهيره ولو ندباً، فلو خرج من جرح في الـعـيـن دم فسال إلى الجانب الآخر منها لا ينقض، لأنه لا يندب تطهيره، كما لا يجب، بخلاف ما لو نزل من الرأس إلى ما صلب من الأنف، لأنه يندب غسله، ولو ربط الجرح فنفذت البـلـة إلـى طــاق لا إلى الخارج، نقض لو كان بحيث لولا الربط سال، لأن القميص لو تردد على الجرح فابتل، لا ينجس ما لم يكن كذلك، ولو تورم رأس الجرح فظهر به قيح ونحوه، لا ينقض ما لم يجاوز الورم. وقوله ( كدم وقيح) إشارة إلى أنه لا فرق بين الدم والقيح والصديد والماء؛ لأنه دم تم نضجه، لأن الدم ينضج فيصير صديداً، ثم يزداد نضجاً فيصير قيحاً، ثم يزداد نضجاً فيصير ماءً. فإذا تم نضجه لا يتغير. فالجرح، والنفطة، وماء الثدي، والسرة، والأذن إذا كان لعلة سواء على الأصح. وعلى هذا قالوا: مــن رمــدت عينه وسال الماء منها، وجب عليه الوضوء، فإذا استمر فلوقت كل صلاة ، وإذا مص القراد فامتلأ دماً، إن كان صغيراً لا ينقض، كما لو مص الذباب والبراغيث، وإن كان كبيراً نقض، كمص العلق، وذلك بحيث لو شرط سال ما مص. (وَ) ينقض الوضوء (قيء طعام، أو ماء، أو علـق) ، وهـو : مـا اشتدت حمرته وجمد وهي سوداء محترقة. ( أوْ مِرَّةٍ) أي: صفراء (إذا مَلاً الفم)، لأنه يكون متنجساً بما في قعر المعدة، بفتح الميم وإسكان العين، فينتقض لقوله : « مَنْ أَصَابَهُ قَيْءٌ أَوْ رُعَافُ أَوْ قَلَسٌ أوْ مَدْي فَلْيَنْصَرف، فَلْيَتَوَضًا، ثُمَّ لِبَيْن عَلَى صَلاتِهِ وَهُوَ فِي ذَلِكَ لا يَتَكَلَّمُ ، وهو مذهب العشرة المبشرين بالجنة ، ومن تابعهم. ولأنه قاءَ فتوضأ » . قال الترمذي: وهو أصح شيء في الباب. ورواه الحاكم في ( المستدرك ) وقال : صحيح على شرط الشيخين ولم يخرجاه ولقوله : « يُعاد الوُضُوءِ مِنْ سَبْعٍ، مِنْ إِقْطَارِ البَوْلِ والدَّمِ السَّائِلِ والقيء، ومِنْ دَسْعَةٍ تَمْلأُ الفَمَ، وَنَوْمِ مضطجع، وقَهْقَهَةِ الرَّجُلِ فِي الصَّلاةِ وخُرُوجِ الدَّمِ ). ولما كان ملء الفم مختلفاً فيه، قال: (وَهُوَ)، أي: ملء الفم ( ما لا ينطبق عليه الفم إلا بتكلف على الأصح)، من التفاسير فيه، وهو رواية الحسـن بــن زيـاد. وقيل: ما لا يمكن الكلام معه. وقيل: أن يزيد على نصف الفم، وقيل: أن يجاوز الفم، وقيل: أن يعجز عن إمساكه، وفرق بين الفم وغيره، حيث شرط للنقض ملء الفم. وأما في نجاسة غيره فبالسيلان، لأن الفم تجاذب فيه دليلان، أحدهما يقتضي كونه ظاهراً، والآخر يقتضي كـونـه بـاطـنـاً حقيقـة وحكماً. أما الحقيقة فلأنه إذا فتح فاه يظهر ، وإذا ضمه يبطن، وأما الحكم: فلأن الصائم إذا أخذ الماء بفمه ثم مجه لم يفسد صومه، كما إذا سال الماء على ظاهر جلده فكان ظاهراً، وإذا ابتلع ريقه ، أيضاً، كما إذا انتقل من زاوية من بطنه إلى أخرى، فكان باطناً فوفرنا على الدليلين حكمهما فقلنا: إذا كثر ،نقض، لأنه يخرج غالباً، حيث لا يقدر الإنسان على ضبطه إلا بكلفة، فاعتبر خارجاً، وإذا قل: لا ينقض فيصير تبعاً للريق.
(And) nullify (wudu): blood, pus, vomit of food or water, or thick blood (clot), or bile, if they fill the mouth. And Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Abbas, Zayd ibn Thabit, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and others among the great Companions and leading Tabi'un like al-Hasan al-Basri and Ibn Sirin. And flow (of blood) from other than the two passageways transmits impurity to a place that is subject to the ruling of purification, i.e., its purification is required, even if recommendably. So if blood exits from a wound in the eye and flows to the other side of it, it does not nullify (wudu), because its purification is not recommended, just as it is not obligatory. Unlike if it descends from the head to the fleshy part of the nose, because washing it is recommended. And if a wound is bandaged and the moisture penetrates to a layer (of cloth) but not to the outside, it nullifies if it would have flowed without the bandage, because if a garment is rubbed on a wound and becomes wet, it does not become impure unless it is like that. And if the head of a wound swells and pus or the like appears in it, it does not nullify unless it exceeds the swelling. And his statement 'blood and pus' indicates that there is no difference between blood, pus, sanious fluid, and water; because it is blood that has matured, for blood matures and becomes sanious fluid, then increases in maturity and becomes pus, then increases in maturity and becomes water. So when it matures it does not change. Then wounds, blisters, breast milk, navel, and ear if due to a cause are all the same according to the correct view. And on this basis they said: Whose eye is inflamed and water flows from it, wudu becomes obligatory upon him; if it continues, (he renews wudu) for each prayer time. And if a tick sucks (blood) and fills with blood: if it is small, it does not nullify, like if a fly or flea sucks; if it is large, it nullifies, like a leech sucking; provided that if it were cut it would flow what was sucked. And (the author said): and wudu is nullified by vomit of food, or water, or thick blood (clot), which is that whose redness intensifies and congeals, and it is black burning, or bile, i.e., yellow bile, if it fills the mouth. Because it becomes impure by what is in the bottom of the stomach, so wudu is nullified due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever is afflicted by vomit or nosebleed or regurgitation or madhiy, let him leave, perform wudu, then complete his prayer, and during that he does not speak' – and this is the madhhab of the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise and those who followed them. And because he vomited and performed wudu. Tirmidhi said: 'This is the most authentic thing on the topic.' And al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak and said: 'Sahih according to the condition of the two Shaykhs (Bukhari and Muslim) but they did not narrate it.' And because of his statement: 'Wudu is repeated from seven (things): from dribbling of urine, flowing blood, vomit, from a full-mouth gulp, sleeping lying down, man's laughter in prayer, and exit of blood.' And since filling the mouth is disputed, he said: 'And it (i.e., filling the mouth) is: that which the mouth does not close over except with difficulty, according to the correct view' – one of the interpretations – and it is the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And it is said: what cannot be spoken with. And it is said: that it exceeds half the mouth. And it is said: that it exceeds the mouth. And it is said: that one is unable to retain it. And a distinction is made between the mouth and other body parts: for nullification, filling the mouth is a condition; for impurity of other parts, it is flow. Because the mouth is subject to two conflicting indicators: one indicates it is external, the other indicates it is internal in reality and ruling. As for reality: because when one opens his mouth it is apparent, and when he closes it, it is hidden. As for ruling: because if a fasting person takes water in his mouth and then spits it out, his fast is not invalidated – just as if water flows on the outer skin, it is external; and if he swallows his saliva, also, as if it moves from one corner of his stomach to another, it is internal. So we gave both indicators their due ruling and said: if it is much, it nullifies, because it usually exits, since one cannot control it except with difficulty, so it is considered external; if it is little, it does not nullify, and becomes a follower of saliva.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on the matn of Nur al-Idah, detailing the nullifiers of wudu that exit from the mouth. The author begins by listing substances: blood, pus, vomit of food or water, thick blood (clot), and bile, all of which nullify wudu if they fill the mouth (mil' al-fam). He then supports this with a strong hadith transmitted by Ibn Majah and others, which is verified as authentic by Tirmidhi and Hakim. The key issue is defining 'mil' al-fam'. The author adopts the view of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: it is that which the mouth cannot close over except with difficulty. Other opinions are mentioned (e.g., inability to speak, exceeding half the mouth, etc.). The reason for the condition of fullness is that the mouth has a dual nature: it is both internal and external. Evidence for externality: opening the mouth reveals its contents; for internality: a fasting person's wudu is not broken by water in the mouth unless swallowed, as the mouth is treated like the interior regarding the fast. Thus, when the quantity is large and cannot be controlled, it is considered external and nullifies wudu; when small, it follows the ruling of saliva and does not nullify. Various applications are discussed: blood from wounds, eye inflammation, tick sucking, etc., all subject to the principle of flow and the amount filling the mouth.

Key Terms
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The quantity that the mouth cannot close over except with difficulty; considered a large amount.
قيء (qay'): Vomiting; the active expulsion of stomach contents.
دم (dam): Blood that exits the body and flows (yasilu), nullifies wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does vomiting nullify wudu?
A) Any amount of vomit nullifies wudu.
B) Vomit nullifies wudu only if it fills the mouth (mil' al-fam).
C) Vomit never nullifies wudu.
D) Vomit nullifies wudu only if it exceeds half the mouth.

Key Takeaway
Blood, pus, and vomit nullify wudu only when they fill the mouth, reflecting the Hanafi school's balanced consideration of the mouth's external and internal aspects.

Taharah — Purification

Scattered Vomit and Blood from the Mouth: Hanafi Ruling
Clarifies the ruling on gathering scattered vomit and blood from the mouth that overcomes saliva.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the gathering of scattered vomit and the condition for blood from the mouth to nullify wudu, and identify the preferred position within the school.

Core Idea
Scattered vomit is counted together only if the cause is one; blood nullifies wudu only if it overcomes or equals saliva.

This lesson examines two specific nullifiers of wudu involving bodily fluids. The first deals with how multiple episodes of vomiting are treated: are they combined or kept separate? The second addresses the effect of blood from the mouth when mixed with saliva. Both rulings rely on careful definition of thresholds and juristic reasoning (istihsan) within the Hanafi school. Understanding these details helps avoid incorrect assumptions about when wudu is broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُجمعُ مُتَفَرِّقُ القَيْءِ إِذا اتحد سببُهُ) عند محمد، وهو الأصح. فإذا كان جملة المتفرق يملأ الفم ،نقض، وإلا فلا ، لأن الأصل إضافة الأحكام إلى الأسباب. وقال أبو يوسف: يجمع إن اتحد المجلس، لأن للمجلس أثراً في جمع المتفرقات. وتفسير اتحاد السبب أن يحصل القيء ثانياً قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان، وإن كان الثـانـي بـعـد سكون النفس كان السبب مختلفاً، وقال « أبو علي الدقاق » : يجمع كيف ما كان، فإذا اتحـدا نـقـض اتفاقاً، وقلبه لا ينقض اتفاقاً. . وإن اتحد المكان لا السبب نقض عند أبي يوسف، وقلبـه عنـد محمـد. وماء فم النائم: إن نزل من الرأس فهو طاهر اتفاقاً. وكذا الصاعد من الجوف على المفتــى بـه. وقيل: إن كان أصفر أو منتناً فهو نجس. (وَ) ينقض الوضوء (دَم) خرج من ذات الفم، كمـا إذا عض علـى يابس، فجرح فمه، وخرج دمه، و غلب على البزاق)، وهو البصاق بمعنى واحـد معروف، (أو ساواه)، استحساناً لأن الغالب سائل بقوة نفسه، وكذا المساوي، لأن أحد الجانبين يوجب النقض، والآخر لا يوجبه، فالأخذ بالاحتياط أولى، ولترجيح الحرام على الحلال عند اجتماعهما.
And scattered vomit is gathered (in total) if its cause is one, according to Muhammad (ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani), and this is the most correct view. So if the sum of the scattered vomit fills the mouth, it nullifies; otherwise not, because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes. And Abu Yusuf said: It is gathered if the place (i.e., session) is one, because the session has an effect in gathering scattered things. And the interpretation of oneness of cause is: the vomiting occurs a second time before the distress (nausea) subsides; if the second occurs after the nausea subsides, the cause is different. And Abu 'Ali al-Daqqaq said: It is gathered however it is. So if the (two episodes) unite (in cause), it definitely nullifies; and if not united, it definitely does not nullify. And if the place is unified but not the cause, it nullifies according to Abu Yusuf, and the opposite according to Muhammad. And water from the mouth of a sleeping person: if it descends from the head, it is pure by agreement. Likewise, if it ascends from the stomach, according to the fatwa. And it is said: if it is yellow or stinking, it is impure. And (the author said): And wudu is nullified by blood that exits from the mouth itself, as when someone bites a dry thing, wounds his mouth, and blood comes out and overcomes the saliva, or equals it, by istihsan (juristic preference), because the dominant (state) is flowing by its own strength, and likewise the equal, because one of the two sides (blood and saliva) necessitates nullification, and the other does not, so taking the precaution is better, and because of preferring the forbidden over the permissible when they combine.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on nullifiers of wudu, focusing on two specific issues. First, the gathering of scattered vomit: if a person vomits multiple times in small amounts, do we add them together to determine if they fill the mouth? Imam Muhammad says we add them only if the vomiting stems from one continuous cause (i.e., the same bout of nausea). For example, if the second vomit occurs before the first bout of nausea subsides, they are considered one event; the total must fill the mouth. Abu Yusuf says we add if the vomiting occurs in the same sitting (majlis), regardless of the cause. The author sides with Muhammad ('al-ashahh'). A fatwa is also given regarding water from a sleeping person's mouth: if it descends from the head (e.g., saliva or mucus) it is pure; if it ascends from the stomach and is yellow or foul, it is impure – this is a more detailed ruling. Second, the ruling on blood from the mouth: if a person bites something dry, wounds his mouth, and blood comes out, it nullifies wudu if the blood either overcomes the saliva (ghalaba) or equals it (sāwā). This is by istihsan, because in dominant cases the blood flows under its own force, and precaution is taken to require wudu. The principle of 'tarjīh al-ḥarām 'alā al-ḥalāl' (preferring the forbidden over the permissible when both combine) is invoked to choose nullification.

Key Terms
جمع متفرق (jam' mutafarriq): Combining separate instances of vomiting into one total to check if they fill the mouth.
البزاق (al-buzāq): Saliva or spittle.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference used to rule that blood overcoming or equalling saliva nullifies wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Scattered vomit is combined only when caused by one continuous nausea; blood from the mouth nullifies wudu only if it overcomes or equals saliva, by istihsan.

Definition of 'Filling the Mouth' for Vomit Nullifying Wudu
Defines the criterion for vomit nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'mil' al-fam' for vomit nullifying wudu and apply the dual-nature reasoning to distinguish between abundant and scanty vomit.

Core Idea
Vomit nullifies wudu only when it fills the mouth—the mouth's dual nature as both external and internal sets a unique threshold.

This passage clarifies a key Hanafi specificity in purity rulings: the criterion for vomit to break wudu. Unlike other impurities that nullify upon flowing, the mouth requires a higher threshold because it is both an external and internal organ. Understanding this definition and its rationale is essential for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue and the Hanafi position
Recognize that the passage addresses when vomit nullifies wudu, and note that the Hanafi school requires the vomit to 'fill the mouth'—a condition not found in other schools.
This is a ruling_with_proof: the author gives multiple definitions and then the rationale.
2
Examine the multiple definitions and note the preferred one
List the various interpretations (cannot speak, more than half, overflows, unable to hold) and identify the most correct: 'ما لا ينطبق عليه الفم إلا بتكلف' (what the mouth cannot cover except with difficulty), from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
The author declares this as 'على الأصح'.
3
Understand the dual-nature argument (talil)
The mouth has two conflicting indications: it is both apparent (when open) and internal (when closed). Evidence: water in mouth during fasting is like external (does not break fast when expelled), but swallowing saliva is like internal. The school balances these: abundant vomit (difficult to control) is treated as external and nullifies; scanty vomit follows saliva and does not.
This reasoning justifies the special threshold for the mouth.
4
Apply the ruling to distinguish abundant from scanty vomit
In practice, decide whether the vomit is so much that the mouth cannot close over it without effort. If yes, wudu is nullified; if not, wudu remains valid.
This is the mufta bihi criterion.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the exact definition
Although Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad agree on the condition of filling the mouth, they differ on the precise measure. The author settles on the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad as the most correct.
The authority_weight is nawadir (rare transmission), but the majority of later scholars adopt this definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو: ما لا ينطبق عليه الفم إلا بتكلف، على الأصح من التفاسير فيه، وهو رواية الحسن بن زياد. وقيل: ما لا يمكن الكلام معه. وقيل: أن يزيد على نصف الفم، وقيل: أن يجاوز الفم، وقيل: أن يعجز عن إمساكه، وفرق بين الفم وغيره، حيث شرط للنقض ملء الفم. وأما في نجاسة غيره فبالسيلان، لأن الفم تجاذب فيه دليلان، أحدهما يقتضي كونه ظاهراً، والآخر يقتضي كونه باطناً حقيقة وحكماً. أما الحقيقة فلأنه إذا فتح فاه يظهر، وإذا ضمه يبطن، وأما الحكم: فلأن الصائم إذا أخذ الماء بفمه ثم مجه لم يفسد صومه، كما إذا سال الماء على ظاهر جلده فكان ظاهراً، وإذا ابتلع ريقه، أيضاً، كما إذا انتقل من زاوية من بطنه إلى أخرى، فكان باطناً فوفرنا على الدليلين حكمهما فقلنا: إذا كثر نقض، لأنه يخرج غالباً، حيث لا يقدر الإنسان على ضبطه إلا بكلفة، فاعتبر خارجاً، وإذا قل: لا ينقض فيصير تبعاً للريق.
It is: what the mouth does not cover except with difficulty, according to the most correct interpretation, and it is the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And it was said: what cannot be spoken with. And it was said: that it exceeds half the mouth. And it was said: that it overflows the mouth. And it was said: that one is unable to hold it. And distinction is made between the mouth and other than it, for it is stipulated for nullification that it fill the mouth. As for other than it (in impurity), it is by flowing, because the mouth has two conflicting indications, one indicating it is apparent, the other indicating it is internal in reality and in ruling. As for reality: because when one opens his mouth it appears, and when he closes it it becomes hidden. As for the ruling: because the fasting person, if he takes water into his mouth and then expels it, does not break his fast, just as if water flowed onto the apparent part of his skin, it is apparent; and if he swallows his saliva, likewise, as if it moved from one corner of his stomach to another, it is internal. So we gave both indications their rulings: we say if it is abundant, it nullifies, because it emerges predominantly, so much so that one cannot control it except with difficulty, so it is considered external; if it is little, it does not nullify, so it becomes a follower of saliva.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the legal criterion for vomit to nullify wudu within the Hanafi school. The author explains that 'filling the mouth' (ملء الفم) means the vomit is so abundant that the mouth cannot contain it without effort. Several interpretations are given, but the most correct is that of Imam Muhammad transmitted from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: it is that which the mouth cannot cover except with difficulty. The passage goes on to justify why the mouth has a higher threshold (filling) compared to other body parts where mere flowing suffices for impurity. This is because the mouth is both apparent (when open) and internal (when closed). This dual nature is evidenced by two seemingly contradictory rulings: water taken into the mouth and then expelled does not break the fast (similar to water on skin, indicating it is external), but swallowing saliva is allowed without breaking the fast (similar to internal movement, indicating it is internal). Balancing these, the school ruled that abundant vomit is considered external and thus nullifies, while little is considered a follower of saliva and does not nullify.

Key Terms
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The quantity of vomit that fills the mouth to the extent that one cannot cover it without difficulty.
تكلف (takalluf): Difficulty; effort that is burdensome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Vomit that the mouth cannot cover except with difficulty
C) Vomit that overflows the mouth
D) Vomit that prevents speech

Key Takeaway
Vomit nullifies wudu only if it reaches the threshold of 'filling the mouth', based on the mouth's dual nature as both external and internal.

Ruling on Scattered Vomit: Collection and Nullification
Addresses whether scattered vomit episodes are combined to reach the threshold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the criteria for combining scattered vomit according to Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, and apply the relied-upon view to determine wudu nullification.

Core Idea
Scattered vomit nullifies wudu only if the total fills the mouth and the cause is one, per the relied-upon view.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether scattered episodes of vomiting should be combined to reach the threshold for nullifying wudu. The relied-upon view of Imam Muhammad ties combination to unity of cause, while Abu Yusuf considers unity of session. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying rulings on wudu invalidation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Consider a person who vomits multiple times in small amounts. Does the total matter? This lesson's dispute centers on when to combine them.
The threshold for nullification is vomiting that fills the mouth.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that scattered vomit is combined only if the cause is the same. This means the second vomit occurs before the nausea settles (قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان).
This is the relied-upon view (الأصح).
3
Explain Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf combines if the vomit occurs in the same session (مجلس), regardless of the cause. He argues that the sitting has an effect in collecting scattered things.
This is a valid alternative within the school.
4
Note the third opinion
Abu Ali al-Daqqaq holds that scattered vomit is always combined, whether cause or session is united. The passage notes that when both are united, all agree on nullification; when neither is united, all agree no nullification.
This opinion is not the relied-upon.
5
Determine the relied-upon view
The author explicitly states that Muhammad's view is الأصح (most correct). Therefore, for fatwa, scattered vomit is combined only if the cause is one.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجمع متفرق القيء إذا اتحد سببه عند محمد، وهو الأصح. فإذا كان جملة المتفرق يملأ الفم نقض، وإلا فلا، لأن الأصل إضافة الأحكام إلى الأسباب. وقال أبو يوسف: يجمع إن اتحد المجلس، لأن للمجلس أثراً في جمع المتفرقات. وتفسير اتحاد السبب أن يحصل القيء ثانياً قبل سكون النفس من الغثيان، وإن كان الثاني بعد سكون النفس كان السبب مختلفاً. وقال أبو علي الدقاق: يجمع كيف ما كان، فإذا اتحدا نقض اتفاقاً، وقلبه لا ينقض اتفاقاً. وإن اتحد المكان لا السبب نقض عند أبي يوسف، وقلبه عند محمد.
And scattered vomit is collected if its cause is one, according to Muhammad, and it is the most correct. So if the total of the scattered (vomit) fills the mouth, it nullifies; otherwise no, because the principle is to attribute rulings to causes. Abu Yusuf said: It is collected if the sitting is one, because the sitting has an effect in collecting scattered things. And the interpretation of unity of cause is that the second vomit occurs before the settling of the breath from nausea; if the second is after the settling of the breath, then the cause is different. And Abu Ali al-Daqqaq said: It is collected however it may be; so if they are united, it nullifies by agreement; and the opposite does not nullify by agreement. And if the place is united but not the cause, it nullifies according to Abu Yusuf, and the opposite according to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific issue within the Hanafi school regarding scattered vomit: if a person vomits multiple times but in small amounts, are these episodes combined to determine whether the total reaches the 'filling the mouth' threshold? Imam Muhammad (the most correct view) holds that they are combined only if the cause is the same—i.e., a second vomiting occurs before the nausea subsides. If the cause is the same, the total is considered; otherwise not. Imam Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, combines if the vomit occurs in the same setting (same session), regardless of cause. Abu Ali al-Daqqaq holds that scattered vomit is always combined. The practical effect: if the total fills the mouth when combined, wudu is nullified; otherwise not. The dispute centers on whether rulings attach to causes or to the context in which the vomit occurs.

Key Terms
اتحاد السبب (ittiḥād al-sabab): Unification of cause: the second vomiting occurs before the nausea subsides, indicating a single episode.
المجلس (al-majlis): The sitting or session in which the vomiting occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only if the vomit is in one continuous stream

Key Takeaway
Scattered vomit nullifies wudu only if the total fills the mouth and the episodes are combined according to the relied-upon criterion of unity of cause.

Blood from the Mouth: Nullification of Wudu When Overwhelming or Equal to Saliva
Ruling on blood from the mouth nullifying wudu when it overwhelms or equals saliva.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood from the mouth nullifying wudu and explain the istihsan reasoning when blood overwhelms or equals saliva.

Core Idea
Blood from the mouth nullifies wudu only if it overwhelms or equals saliva; caution tips the balance when evidence is equal.

In this passage, the author of Nur al-Idah explains a nuanced ruling regarding blood exiting from the mouth. While the general principle is that liquid impurities exiting the body nullify wudu, the mouth presents a unique case because saliva is inherently pure. The Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to determine when the presence of blood triggers nullification. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning and the conditions that must be met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? Blood exiting from the mouth. The ruling is that it nullifies wudu under specific conditions.
2
Identify the dalil
What proof is cited? Istihsan (juristic preference) based on the principle of caution (ihtiyat) and giving precedence to prohibition over permission when they combine.
The dalil is not a direct text but a rational derivation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof lead to the ruling? For overwhelming blood, it flows by its own force like other impure liquids, so it nullifies. For equal amounts, there is a conflict of analogy: one side suggests nullification (blood as impurity), the other suggests not (saliva as pure). Caution requires nullification to avoid praying in impurity.
The rule 'when halal and haram combine, give precedence to haram' is invoked.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that any impure substance exiting the body nullifies wudu, regardless of quantity relative to saliva. They do not make the distinction based on dominance.
This difference affects practical application: a small amount of blood in saliva may break wudu in Shafi'i but not in Hanafi if saliva dominates.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) in the school, based on the reasoning provided. It is consistent with the zahir al-riwayah.
No intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) ينقض الوضوء (دَم) خرج من ذات الفم، كما إذا عض على يابس، فجرح فمه، وخرج دمه، وغلب على البزاق، أو ساواه، استحساناً لأن الغالب سائل بقوة نفسه، وكذا المساوي، لأن أحد الجانبين يوجب النقض، والآخر لا يوجبه، فالأخذ بالاحتياط أولى، ولترجيح الحرام على الحلال عند اجتماعهما.
And blood that exits from the mouth, such as when one bites on something dry and his mouth bleeds, and the blood overwhelms the saliva or equals it, nullifies wudu by istihsan, because the overwhelming (blood) flows by its own force, and likewise the equal, because one side requires nullification and the other does not, so caution is preferable, and to give precedence to the prohibited over the permissible when they are combined.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding blood from the mouth. According to the matn, wudu is nullified by blood that either overwhelms the saliva (i.e., is more in quantity) or equals it. The basis is istihsan (juristic preference). The reasoning: if the blood is dominant, it flows by its own force, making it a liquid impurity that exits the body. If it equals saliva, there is doubt: one indication suggests nullification (it is like flowing blood) and another suggests not (saliva is not impurity). In case of doubt, caution (احتياط) is applied, and the ruling of nullification is given because the prohibition (of being in a state of impurity) is preferred over the permission. This principle is that when halal and haram combine, one leans towards haram to avoid the prohibition. The passage also implies that if saliva dominates, wudu is not nullified.

Key Terms
البزاق (al-buzāq): Saliva, spit from the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Wudu is not nullified because saliva is pure
C) Wudu is nullified only if blood dominates
D) Wudu is nullified only if the blood is from a head wound

Key Takeaway
Blood from the mouth nullifies wudu only when it overwhelms or equals saliva, based on the principle of caution (istihsan) when halal and haram combine.

Sleep and Raising of the Seat: Nullifiers of Wudu
Rulings with proof on sleep and seat raise as nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which sleep nullifies wudu according to the Hanafi school, and identify the distinction between heavy and light drowsiness.

Core Idea
Sleep nullifies wudu only when the seat is not firmly placed; light drowsiness does not nullify.

In this passage, the author explains two nuanced nullifiers of wudu: sleep and the raising of the seat. The key principle is that wudu is nullified when the sphincter loses its control, which is typically associated with deep sleep in an unstable position. The hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) serves as the proof, and the scholar Qadi Khan clarifies the distinction between intentional and unintentional sleep. Understanding these details ensures that one does not invalidate salah unnecessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is sleep. The ruling is that it nullifies wudu (yanquḍ al-wuḍūʾ) under specific conditions: when the seat (maqʿadah) is not firmly placed on the ground. This includes lying down, leaning, or being on one's face or back.
The author uses the phrase 'على الصحيح' (according to the correct view), indicating this is the relied-upon position.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'العينان وكاء السه، فإذا نامت العين استطلق الوكاء' (The eyes are the drawstring of the anus; when the eye sleeps, the drawstring is released). This is a ʾaḥad hadith, thus zannī al-thubūt.
The hadith is cited to establish that sleep leads to relaxation of the sphincter, implying that wind may be released.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The wajh al-dalālah lies in the metaphor: 'وكاء السه' (drawstring of the anus) is the muscle control. When the eye sleeps (deep sleep), the drawstring is released, meaning control is lost. This indicates that deep sleep causes the emission of wind, which nullifies wudu.
The author also notes that sleep itself is not the ḥadath (impurity); rather, the ḥadath is what the sleeping person cannot avoid. The apparent cause (sleep) is used as a substitute, similar to travel being a substitute for hardship in shortening prayer.
4
Note the distinctions
The passage distinguishes between intentional sleep (تعمد) and unintentional sleep. Intentional sleep in prostration nullifies wudu and invalidates salah, but in standing or bowing it does not. Unintentional sleep in any position does not nullify according to ẓāhir al-riwāyah. Drowsiness (نعاس) is also distinguished: light drowsiness (where one can hear) does not nullify, while heavy drowsiness (where one cannot hear) nullifies when lying down.
These distinctions come from Qadi Khan and are part of the Ḥanafī school's detailed rulings.
5
Consider the exception and the second nullifier
The Prophet's (ﷺ) sleep lying down is an exception because of his special quality: 'My eyes sleep but my heart does not sleep.' Additionally, raising the seat of a sleeping person (while still asleep) nullifies wudu even if he does not fall, according to the apparent madhhab of Abū Ḥanīfa. However, if he awakens before his side touches the ground, it does not nullify according to another narration.
This second ruling is based on the loss of strength and its successor (zawāl al-quwwa wa khalfa-hā).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونوم لم تتمكن فيه المقعدة من الأرض، كنوم مضطجع، ومتروك ومنكب على وجهه ومستلق على قفاه ونوم مريض يصلي مضطجعاً بالإيماء ناقض على الصحيح. لقوله : ( العينان وكاء السه، فإذا نامت العين استطلق الوكاء ). وفيه التنبيه على أن الناقض ليس النوم، لأنه ليس بحدث، وإنما الحدث ما لا يخلو النائم عنه، فأقيم السبب الظاهر مقامه، كما في السفر. وإذا تعمد النوم في الصلاة، فإن كان في قيامه، أو ركوعه، لا تنتقض طهارته، وإن تعمده في السجود تنتقض طهارته، وتبطل صلاته. وإن لم يتعمد فنام قائماً، أو راكعاً، أو ساجداً، لا يكون حدثاً، في ظاهر الرواية قاله « قاضي خان». وقيدنا بالنوم احترازاً عن النعاس. والنعاس على نوعين: ثقيل وهو : حدث في حالة الاضطجاع، وخفيف، وهو : ليس بحدث فيها. والفاصل بينهما: أنه يسمع ما يقال عنده فهو خفيف، وإلا فهو ثقيل. تنبيه: النوم مضطجعاً ليس ناقضاً في حق النبي لأنه من خصوصياته، لما ورد عنه : « إن عيني تنامان ولا ينام قلبي ) (و) ينقض الوضوء (ارتفاع مقعدة) قاعد (نائم) على الأرض (قبل انتباهه، وإن لم يسقط على الأرض ( في الظاهر ) من مذهب أبي حنيفة، لزوال القوة وخلفها وعنه: إن انتبه قبل وصول جنبه إلى الأرض، أو عند إصابتها بلا فصل لم ينقض.
And sleep in which the seat is not firmly placed on the ground, such as lying down, leaning, and on one's face and on the back, and the sleep of a sick person praying lying down with gestures, nullifies (wudu) according to the correct view, because of his saying: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus; when the eye sleeps, the drawstring is released.' And in it is an indication that sleep is not the actual nullifier, because it is not an event itself; rather, the event is what the sleeping person cannot avoid, so the apparent cause is established as its substitute, as in travel. And if one deliberately sleeps in prayer: if it is in standing or bowing, his purification is not nullified; if he deliberately sleeps in prostration, his purification is nullified and his prayer invalidated. And if he does not intend it and sleeps standing, bowing, or prostrating, it is not an event in the apparent narration, as stated by Qadi Khan. And we restrict (the ruling) to sleep to exclude drowsiness. And drowsiness is of two types: heavy, which is an event in the state of lying down, and light, which is not an event in it. And the distinction between them is: if one hears what is said near him, it is light; otherwise it is heavy. Note: Sleep lying down is not a nullifier with respect to the Prophet, because it is among his specialities, due to what is reported from him: 'My eyes sleep but my heart does not sleep.' And it nullifies wudu: the raising of the seat of a sleeping person sitting on the ground before he awakens, even if he does not fall to the ground in the apparent (position) according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, because of the loss of strength and its successor. And from him: if he awakens before his side reaches the ground, or at the moment of contact without delay, it does not nullify.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage covers two related nullifiers of wudu: sleep and the raising of the seat of a sleeping person. For sleep, the key condition is that the seat (buttocks) is not firmly pressed on the ground, meaning the person is lying down or in a position where the seat is not stable, such that the sphincter relaxes. The hadith 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus' indicates that sleep, especially deep sleep, leads to loss of control and potential release of gas, so wudu is nullified. However, not all sleep nullifies: intentional sleep in prostration does, but in standing or bowing it does not, because the seat is still somewhat secure. Involuntary sleep in any position does not nullify according to the ẓāhir al-riwāyah. Drowsiness (نعاس) is distinguished from sleep: if one hears what is said, it is light drowsiness and does not nullify; if not, it is heavy and nullifies when lying down. Also, the Prophet's sleep is an exception as his heart remains awake. For the second ruling, if a sleeping person's seat rises from the ground (e.g., in sleep), even if he doesn't fall, wudu is nullified according to Abu Hanifa because the seat loses its stable position. But if he awakens before his side touches the ground or at the moment of contact without delay, it does not nullify according to another narration.

Key Terms
النوم (al-nawm): Sleep as a natural state that leads to loss of consciousness and sphincter control; different from drowsiness (نعاس).
النعاس (al-nuʿās): Drowsiness, of two types: heavy (nullifies when lying down) and light (does not nullify).
ارتفاع مقعدة (irtifāʿ maqʿadah): Raising of the seat (buttocks) of a sleeping person from the ground.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of sleep nullifies wudu?
A) Sleep in any position
B) Sleep only when lying down on the back
C) Sleep in a position where the seat is not firmly placed on the ground
D) Sleep only if accompanied by dreaming

Key Takeaway
Sleep nullifies wudu only if the seat is not firmly placed on the ground; drowsiness is distinguished by the ability to hear and does not nullify unless heavy and lying down.

Nullification by Unconsciousness, Insanity, and Intoxication
Commentary on three states that nullify wudu completely

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu nullification by unconsciousness, insanity, and intoxication, and explain the proof from the hadith 'the eyes are the drawstring of the anus.'

Core Idea
These three states nullify wudu in all postures because they are more severe than sleep and inevitably cause the exit of wind.

In this lesson, we examine three conditions that completely nullify wudu: unconsciousness (ighmā'), insanity (junūn), and intoxication (sukr). The commentator defines each state and explains why the ruling applies in all bodily postures—standing, bowing, prostration, and lying down. The key evidence is the same hadith used for sleep: the eyes are the drawstring of the anus; since these states are deeper than sleep and cannot be interrupted by waking, they necessarily lead to the exit of wind, thus nullifying wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three nullifiers
Note the three states listed: unconsciousness, insanity, and intoxication. The passage will define each and state the ruling that they nullify wudu.
These are additional to sleep, which was covered earlier.
2
Analyze the definitions
Read the definitions: unconsciousness removes strength and conceals intellect; insanity removes intellect but increases strength; intoxication is a lightness causing swaying and stammering, due to loss of the restraining force.
Each definition emphasizes the loss of control, which is the basis for nullification.
3
Trace the proof (dalil)
The proof is the same hadith: 'الْعَيْنَانِ وِكَاءُ السَّهِ' (the eyes are the drawstring of the anus). Since these states are more severe than sleep—they do not cease by waking—they inevitably lead to the release of wind, thus nullifying wudu in all positions.
The cause (exit of wind) is hidden, so the ruling is tied to these apparent states.
4
Understand the superiority over sleep
The text argues these states are 'above sleep' because the sleeper can be woken, but someone unconscious, insane, or intoxicated does not respond to waking. Hence, the nullification applies in every posture.
This is the key distinction that justifies the blanket ruling.
5
Note the physiological explanation
The commentator gives a physiological reason for intoxication: wine's effect reaches the chest, blocking the light of the intellect from the heart, hence it is called 'sukr' (barrier). This reinforces the loss of control.
This is an additional insight, not a separate legal ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإغماء، وجنون، وسكر، وقهقهه بالغ يقظان في صلاة ذاتِ ركوع وسجود، ولو تعمد الخروج بها من الصلاة، وإغماء)، وهو مرض يزيل القوى، ويستر العقل. (و) ينقضه (جُنُـون)، وهو مرض يزيل الحجا، ويزيد القوى. (و) ينقضه (سُكر)، وهو: خفة تعتري الإنسان، ويظهر أثرها في مشيته بالتمايل وتلعثم كلامه، لزوال القوة الماسكة. وهذه من الأمور التي يدار الحكم فيها على أسبابها الظاهرة، لخفاء العلة التي هي خروج الناقض، والأصل فيها ما قدمناه من قوله : الْعَيْنَانِ وِكَاءُ السَّهِ. وهذه الأحوال حدث في كل الصور : القيام والركوع والسجود والاضطجاع، لأنها فوق النوم، لعدم زوالها بالتنبيه، والنائم إذا نبهته انتبه. والعقل في الرأس، وشعاعه في الصدر والقلب، أو بالقلب، فالقلب يهتدي بنوره، لتدبير الأمور وتمييز الحسن من القبيح، فإذا شرب الخمر خلص أثرها إلى الصدر، فحال بينه وبين نور العقل فبقي الصدر مظلماً، فلم ينتفع القلب بنور العقل، فسمي لذلك سكراً، لأنه سكر حاجز بينه وبين العقل.
And unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, and loud laughter of an adult, conscious in a prayer with bowing and prostration, even if he intentionally exits the prayer by it. (Unconsciousness): it is a disease that removes strength and conceals intellect. (And) it is nullified by (insanity), which is a disease that removes intellect and increases strength. (And) it is nullified by (intoxication), which is a lightness that affects a person, and its effect appears in his walking with swaying and stammering of speech, due to loss of the restraining force. And these are among the matters whose ruling is based on their apparent causes, because the cause (exit of the nullifier) is hidden. The basis for them is what we presented of his saying: 'The eyes are the drawstring of the anus.' And these states are an event in all postures: standing, bowing, prostration, and lying down, because they are above sleep, since they do not cease by waking. The sleeper if you wake him, he wakes. And the intellect is in the head, and its ray is in the chest and heart, or with the heart, so the heart is guided by its light to manage affairs and distinguish good from evil. When one drinks wine, its effect goes to the chest, and it interposes between him and the light of the intellect, so the chest remains dark, and the heart does not benefit from the light of the intellect. Therefore, it is called intoxication because it is a barrier between him and the intellect.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the commentary on the ruling that unconsciousness (ighmā'), insanity (junūn), and intoxication (sukr) nullify wudu. These states are mentioned in the matn along with loud laughter. The commentator defines each: unconsciousness is a disease that removes strength and conceals intellect; insanity removes intellect and increases strength; intoxication is a lightness affecting the person, seen in swaying and stammering, due to loss of the restraining force. The ruling is based on the same hadith about the eyes being the drawstring; since the cause (exit of wind) is hidden, the ruling is tied to these apparent states. Importantly, these states nullify wudu in all prayer positions (standing, bowing, prostration, lying down) because they are more severe than sleep—the sleeper can be woken, but these states do not yield to waking. The commentator also provides a physiological explanation of intoxication: wine's effect reaches the chest and blocks the light of the intellect from reaching the heart, hence it is called 'sukr' (barrier).

Key Terms
الإغماء (al-ighmā'): A disease that removes strength and conceals intellect.
الجنون (al-junūn): A disease that removes intellect and increases strength.
السكر (al-sukr): A lightness affecting the person, causing swaying and stammering due to loss of the restraining force; a barrier between the intellect and the heart.

Nullification by Loud Laughter in Prayer
Ruling with proof: loud laughter nullifies wudu and prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loud laughter in prayer, distinguish it from ordinary laughter and smiling, and apply the conditions and exceptions.

Core Idea
Loud laughter nullifies both prayer and wudu; but only in a complete prayer with bowing and prostration.

A person laughing loudly during prayer — does it break the prayer only, or also the wudu? The Hanafi school distinguishes three levels of audible reaction: smiling (no effect), ordinary laughter (breaks prayer only), and loud laughter (breaks both). This lesson unpacks the ruling, its basis in a prophetic hadith, and the conditions that limit its application.

Guided Path
1
1. Define the three levels
Read the definitions of qahqaha, dahk, and tabassum. Note which nullifies what.
Qahqaha = heard by self and neighbors; Dahk = heard only by self; Tabassum = not heard.
2
2. Identify the conditions
The ruling applies only to a praying adult, conscious, in a complete prayer (with ruku and sujud). Identify each condition and its rationale.
Excluded: child, sleeping, funeral prayer, sajdat al-tilawah.
3
3. Locate the dalil
Find the hadith about the blind man falling. Explain how the Prophet's command to repeat wudu and prayer proves nullification of both.
The hadith is the primary evidence (dalil naqli).
4
4. Examine the disagreement on wudu in ghusl
Scholars agreed qahqaha nullifies prayer and does not nullify ghusl. They differed on nullifying the wudu implicit in ghusl. Qadi Khan upheld nullification as punishment.
This is a secondary issue within Hanafi school.
5
5. Apply to borderline cases
Consider the case where the person intentionally laughs after completing tashahhud to exit prayer. The commentary states it still nullifies. Understand why.
The act remains within prayer until the salutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) ينقض الوضوء (قَهْقَهَةُ) مصل (بالغ) عمداً كانت أو سهواً، وهي ما يكون مسموعاً له ولجيرانه، واحترز بها عن الضحك، وهو ما يكون مسموعاً له فقط. فإنه يبطل الصلاة خاصة، وعن التبسم، وهو ما لم يكن مسموعاً له، فإنه لا حكم له، ولو بدت منه أسنانه. وقيد بالبالغ، لأن الصبي لا ينتقض وضوؤه بالقهقهة، لأنه ليس من أهل الزجر. وقيل: ينتقض ، (يَقْظَانَ) لا نائم على الأصح (في) كل (صلاة) كاملة وهي: ( ذَاتِ ركوع وسجود) بالأصالة. ولو كانت بالإيماء، سواء كان متوضئاً أو متيمماً أو مغتسلاً. واتفقوا على أنها لا تبطل الغسل، واتفقوا على بطلان الصلاة بها، واختلفوا في نقضها الوضوء الذي في ضمن الغسل، وصحح قاضي خان وأمثاله النقض، عقوبة له، لكونها ليست حدثاً حقيقياً، فلا يلزم القول بتجزئة الطهارة، واحترزنا بالكاملة عن صلاة الجنازة، وسجدة التلاوة، لأن الأمر ورد بذلك في صلاة كاملة، لما روي « أنَّ أَعْمَى تَرَدَّى فِي حفيرة لماء المطر بــاب المسجد، والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يُصَلِّي بأصحَابِهِ فَضَحِكَ بعضُ مَنْ كَانَ يُصلي مَعَهُ عليه الصلاة والسلام، فأمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مَنْ ضَحِكَ مِنْهُم قَهْقَهَةٌ، أن يعيد الوضوء والصلاةَ » ، ولعل الضاحك كان من الشبان أو الأعراب، أو المنافقين، لا من كبار الصحابة والمهاجرين والأنصار. (وَ) القهقهة ناقضة (لَوْ تَعَمَّدَ) فاعلها (الخُرُوجَ بِهَا مِنَ الصَّلاةِ)، بعدما قعد قدر التشهد، ولم يبق إلا السلام، أو كان في سجود السهو، أو بعدما توضأ لسبق
And loud laughter nullifies wudu - of a praying adult, intentionally or forgetfully. And it is what is heard by him and his neighbors. He distinguished it from laughter (dahk), which is only heard by him; that invalidates prayer only. And from smiling (tabassum), which is not heard by him; it has no ruling, even if his teeth appear. And he restricted it to an adult because the wudu of a child is not nullified by loud laughter, since he is not subject to deterrence. And it is said: it does nullify. (Conscious), not asleep, according to the most correct view. (In) every (complete) prayer, which has bowing and prostration originally. Even if it is performed with gestures, whether the person is in a state of wudu or tayammum or ghusl. And they agreed that it does not invalidate ghusl, and they agreed that it invalidates prayer by it, and they differed on whether it nullifies the wudu that is implicit in ghusl. Qadi Khan and his likes corrected the nullification as a punishment, since it is not a real event, so one does not have to say that purity is divided. And we restricted it to a complete prayer to exclude funeral prayer and prostration of recitation, because the command came regarding that in a complete prayer, due to what was narrated: 'A blind man fell into a pit of rainwater at the door of the mosque while the Prophet was praying with his companions, and some of those praying with him laughed loudly. So the Prophet ordered those who laughed loudly to repeat wudu and prayer.' And perhaps the laugher was among the youths or Bedouins or hypocrites, not among the senior Companions and Muhajirun and Ansar. And loud laughter is a nullifier even if the doer intentionally exits the prayer by it, after he sat for the amount of tashahhud and nothing remained except the salutation, or if he was in the prostration of forgetfulness, or after he performed wudu for something that preceded...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is the commentary on the ruling that loud laughter (qahqaha) during a complete prayer (with bowing and prostration) nullifies both the prayer and wudu. The commentator defines loud laughter as audible to the laugher and his neighbors, distinguishing it from ordinary laughter (only heard by the laugher) which invalidates prayer but not wudu, and from smiling (not heard) which has no effect. The ruling applies to an adult who is conscious, not asleep. It applies to any complete prayer, including those performed with gestures due to illness, and whether one has wudu, tayammum, or ghusl. Scholars agreed that loud laughter invalidates the prayer and does not invalidate ghusl, but differed on whether it nullifies the wudu that is part of ghusl. Qadi Khan upheld nullification as a punishment, since it is not a real hadath. The commentary limits it to complete prayers (excluding funeral prayer and prostration of recitation) based on a hadith where the Prophet ordered those who laughed loudly during prayer to repeat wudu and prayer. The commentator also notes that the narration likely involved less notable individuals. Finally, even if one intentionally leaves the prayer by laughing after having sat for tashahhud and only remaining salutation, or during sajdat al-sahw, or after performing wudu for something earlier, the laughter still nullifies.

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter that is heard by the person and his neighbors.
الضحك (al-dahk): Laughter heard only by the laugher; it invalidates prayer but not wudu.
التبسم (al-tabassum): Smiling that is not heard; it has no effect on prayer or wudu.
صلاة كاملة (salat kāmila): A complete prayer that originally includes bowing (ruku) and prostration (sujud).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Loud laughter (qahqaha) during a complete prayer nullifies both wudu and prayer, based on the Prophet's command to repeat both.

Nullifiers of Wudu: Unconsciousness, Insanity, and Intoxication
Defines three states that nullify wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ighma', junun, and sukr, explain why they nullify wudu, and apply the principle that they are stronger than sleep.

Core Idea
Loss of control nullifies wudu: unconsciousness, insanity, and intoxication are hadath because they surpass sleep in removing control.

We have learned that sleep nullifies wudu because it relaxes the sphincters. The Hanafi masters now present three states that are even more severe: unconsciousness, insanity, and intoxication. This lesson explains their definitions and why they are considered greater hadath events.

Guided Path
1
Identify each state and its definition
Read the definitions of ighma' (unconsciousness), junun (insanity), and sukr (intoxication) from the text. Note the key characteristics: ighma' removes strength and veils intellect; junun removes intellect and increases strength; sukr causes lightness that affects coordination and speech.
2
Determine the shared characteristic
All three involve loss of intellectual control but may increase physical strength. The underlying cause is the removal of the 'retaining force' (al-quwwa al-masika), leading to loss of control over bodily functions.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The ruling is based on the principle that these states are stronger than sleep: a sleeping person can be awakened, but these states persist despite being disturbed. The hadith 'The two eyes are the string of the anus' indicates that lack of control nullifies wudu.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and others, indicating that sleep relaxes the sphincters; these states are more severe.
4
Apply the ruling
In any prayer posture (standing, bowing, prostrating, lying), these states nullify wudu. The person must renew wudu before praying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإغماء، وجنون، وسكر، وقهقهه بالغ يقظان في صلاة ذاتِ ركوع وسجود، ولو تعمد الخروج بـهـا مـن الصلاة، (و) ينقض الوضوء (إغماء)، وهو مرض يزيل القوى، ويستر العقل. (و) ينقضه (جُنُـون)، وهو مرض يزيل الحجا)، ويزيد القوى. (و) ينقضه (سُكر)، وهو: خفة تعتري الإنسان، ويظهر أثرها في مشيته بالتمايل وتلعثم كلامه، لزوال القوة الماسكة. وهذه من الأمور التي يدار الحكم فيها على أسبابها الظاهرة، لخفاء العلة التي هي خروج الناقض، والأصل فيها ما قدمناه من قوله : الْعَيْنَانِ وِكَاءُ السَّهِ» (۳). وهذه الأحوال حدث في كل الصور : القيام والركوع والسجود والاضطجاع، لأنها فوق النوم، لعدم زوالها بالتنبيه، والنائم إذا نبهته انتبه. والعقل في الرأس، وشعاعه في الصدر والقلب، أو بالقلب، فالقلب يهتدي بنوره، لتدبير الأمور وتمييز الحسن من القبيح، فإذا شرب الخمر خلص أثرها إلى الصدر، فحال بينه وبين نور العقل فبقي الصدر مظلماً، فلم ينتفع القلـب بـنـور العقل، فسمي لذلك سكراً، لأنه سكر حاجز بينه وبين العقل. (و) ينقض الوضوء (قَهْقَهَةُ) مصل (بالغ) عمداً كانت أو سهواً، وهي ما يكون مسموعاً له ولجيرانه، واحترز بها عن الضحك، وهـو مـا يكون مسموعاً له فقط. فإنه يبطل الصلاة خاصة، وعن التبسم، وهو ما لم يكن مسموعاً له، فإنه لا حكم له، ولو بدت منه أسنانه. وقيد بالبالغ، لأن الصبي لا ينتقض وضوؤه بالقهقهة، لأنـه ليـس مـن أهل الزجر. وقيل: ينتقض ، (يَقْظَانَ) لا نائم على الأصح (في) كل (صلاة) كاملة وهي: ( ذَاتِ ركوع وسجود) بالأصالة. ولو كانت بالإيماء، سواء كان متوضئاً أو متيمماً أو مغتسلاً. واتفقوا علــى أنها لا تبطل الغسل، واتفقوا على بطلان الصلاة بها، واختلفوا في نقضها الوضوء الذي في ضمن الغسل، وصحح قاضي خان وأمثاله النقض، عقوبة له، لكونها ليست حدثاً حقيقياً، فلا يلزم القول بتجزئة الطهارة، واحترزنا بالكاملة عن صلاة الجنازة، وسجدة التلاوة، لأن الأمر ورد بذلك في صلاة كاملة، لما روي « أنَّ أَعْمَى تَرَدَّى فِي حفيرة لماء المطر بــاب المسجد، والنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يُصَلِّي بأصحَابِهِ فَضَحِكَ بعضُ مَنْ كَانَ يُصلي مَعَهُ عليه الصلاة والسلام، فأمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مَنْ ضَحِكَ مِنْهُم قَهْقَهَةٌ، أن يعيد الوضوء والصلاةَ » (۳) ، ولعل الضاحك كان من الشبان أو الأعراب، أو المنافقين، لا من كبار الصحابة والمهاجرين والأنصار. (وَ) القهقهة ناقضة (لَوْ تَعَمَّدَ) فاعلها (الخُرُوجَ بِهَا مِنَ الصَّلاةِ)، بعدما قعد قدر التشهد، ولم يبق إلا السلام، أو كان في سجود السهو، أو بعدما توضأ لسبق حدث، قبل أن يبني لوجود القهقهة في حرمة الصلاة. وأما صحة الصلاة . بوجود القهقهة بعدما قعد قدر التشهد، فلكونها لم يبق من فرائضها شيء. وترك لفظ السلام لا يضر في الصحة.
And unconsciousness, insanity, intoxication, and qahqaha (laugh out loud) of an adult, awake in a prayer that has bowing and prostration, even if he intentionally exits the prayer by it, (and) unconsciousness nullifies wudu, and it is a disease that removes strength and veils the intellect. (And) insanity nullifies it, and it is a disease that removes intellect and increases strength. (And) intoxication nullifies it, and it is: lightness that strikes a person, and its effect appears in his walk with swaying and his speech stammering, due to the removal of the retaining force. And these are among matters in which the ruling is based on their apparent causes, due to the hidden nature of the effective cause which is the exit of the nullifier. And the basis for them is what we previously presented from his statement: 'The two eyes are the string of the anus.' (3). And these states are a hadath (ritual impurity) in all postures: standing, bowing, prostration, and lying down, because they are above sleep, due to their not being removed by waking; the sleeping person, if you wake him, he wakes up. And the intellect is in the head, and its ray is in the chest and heart, or by the heart, so the heart is guided by its light to manage affairs and distinguish good from evil. When one drinks wine, its effect penetrates to the chest, so it interposes between it and the light of the intellect, so the chest remains dark, and the heart does not benefit from the light of the intellect, so it is called intoxication because it is a barrier between it and the intellect. (And) qahqaha of a praying person nullifies wudu, whether intentional or forgetful, and it is what is heard by him and his neighbors; he distinguished it from d-h-k (laughter), which is what is heard only by him, for it invalidates the prayer specifically; and from tabassum (smiling), which is what is not heard by him, for it has no ruling, even if his teeth appear. And it is restricted to an adult, because the wudu of a child is not nullified by qahqaha, because he is not among those who are rebuked. And it is said: it is nullified. (Awake) not sleeping, on the most correct view, (in) every complete prayer, which is: (having bowing and prostration) inherently. And if it were by gesture, whether he had wudu or tayammum or had performed ghusl. And they agreed that it does not nullify ghusl, and they agreed on the invalidation of the prayer by it, and they differed about its nullification of the wudu that is within the ghusl. Qadi Khan and similar scholars affirmed the nullification as a punishment, because it is not a real hadath, so it does not necessitate the division of purity. And we restricted to complete prayer to exclude funeral prayer and prostration of recitation, because the command came for that in a complete prayer, as it is narrated: 'A blind man fell into a pit of rainwater at the door of the mosque while the Prophet ﷺ was praying with his companions, and some who were praying with him laughed, so the Prophet ﷺ ordered those who laughed out loud to repeat the wudu and the prayer.' (3) And perhaps the one who laughed was among the youths or Bedouins or hypocrites, not among the senior Companions, Muhajirun, and Ansar. (And) qahqaha is a nullifier (even if) the doer (intentionally exits the prayer by it), after he sat for the amount of tashahhud, and nothing remains except the salutation, or he was in the prostration of forgetfulness, or after he performed wudu due to a previous hadath, before he builds on it, because the qahqaha occurred in the sanctity of prayer. As for the validity of the prayer with the occurrence of qahqaha after sitting for the amount of tashahhud, it is because nothing of its obligatory elements remains. And leaving the words of salutation does not harm validity.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, explains four nullifiers of wudu: unconsciousness (ighma'), insanity (junun), intoxication (sukr), and laughing out loud (qahqaha) during prayer. The author first defines each: unconsciousness is a disease that removes strength and veils intellect; insanity removes intellect and increases strength; intoxication is a lightness with visible effects on walk and speech. These are treated as hadath (ritual impurity) in all postures because they are stronger than sleep—they are not removed by waking. The underlying principle is based on the hadith 'The two eyes are the string of the anus' implying loss of control. Regarding qahqaha, the text distinguishes it from ordinary laughter (only heard by the person, invalidates prayer but not wudu) and smiling (no ruling). Qahqaha nullifies wudu only in a complete prayer (having ruku' and sujud), for an adult who is awake, whether intentional or forgetful. A hadith is mentioned: a blind man fell into a pit, some companions laughed, and the Prophet ordered them to repeat wudu and prayer. The author notes that the laughers were likely not from the senior Companions. The passage also discusses edge cases: qahqaha after tashahhud does not invalidate the prayer because only the salutation remains, and leaving the salutation does not harm validity.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighma'): A disease that removes strength and veils intellect, causing loss of consciousness.
جنون (junun): A disease that removes intellect (hija) and increases strength (quwwa).
سكر (sukr): Intoxication: a state of lightness affecting coordination and speech due to removal of the retaining force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a nullifier of wudu according to the Hanafi school as covered in this lesson?
A) Unconsciousness (ighma')
B) Insanity (junun)
C) Intoxication (sukr)
D) Forgetting to say the basmalah

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness, insanity, and intoxication nullify wudu because they involve a loss of control that surpasses sleep, as they are not easily reversed.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Laughing Out Loud (Qahqaha) in Prayer: Nullification of Wudu and Prayer
Details the ruling of qahqaha in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qahqaha, distinguish it from ordinary laughter and smiling, state when it nullifies wudu and prayer, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ghusl.

Core Idea
Qahqaha nullifies wudu in a complete prayer, but not in funeral prayer or prostration of recitation, and it is a punishment, not a real hadath.

In the previous lesson we covered loss-of-control states. This lesson focuses on a unique nullifier: laughing out loud during prayer. The Hanafi school treats qahqaha as a nullifier of both wudu and prayer, but with specific conditions. We will examine the definitions, the proof from hadith, and the scholarly difference about its effect on ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Define qahqaha and distinguish it from dahik and tabassum
Read the text: qahqaha is laughter audible to the person and his neighbors; dahik is audible only to the person (invalidates prayer but not wudu); tabassum is silent smiling (no effect).
The text says 'wa ihtiraza biha an al-dahik' - he distinguished it from dahik.
2
Identify the conditions for nullification
Qahqaha nullifies wudu only if the person is an adult, awake, and in a complete prayer (with ruku' and sujud). The text also mentions that it nullifies even if the person intentionally exits the prayer after sitting for the tashahhud.
The condition of 'complete prayer' excludes funeral prayer and prostration of recitation.
3
Note the exceptions and special cases
The text excludes: funeral prayer (salat al-janazah), prostration of recitation (sajdat al-tilawah), and the case after sitting for tashahhud (only the salutation remains, so prayer is still valid but wudu is nullified). Also, the nullification applies whether the qahqaha is intentional or forgetful.
Also, qahqaha does not nullify ghusl (major ablution), but there is a dispute about the wudu within ghusl.
4
Analyze the proof
The text cites the hadith of the blind man who fell into a pit, causing some companions to laugh. The Prophet ordered those who laughed out loud to repeat wudu and prayer. This command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub).
The author also speculates that the laughers were not from the senior companions to avoid implying any flaw in them.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is disagreement about whether qahqaha nullifies the wudu that is performed as part of ghusl. Qadi Khan and others affirm nullification as a punishment, while others disagree. The relied-upon view is that it does not nullify the wudu within ghusl because it is not a real hadath.
The text says 'wa ikhtalafu fi naqdiha al-wudu alladhi fi dam al-ghusl'.

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, explains four nullifiers of wudu: unconsciousness (ighma'), insanity (junun), intoxication (sukr), and laughing out loud (qahqaha) during prayer. The author first defines each: unconsciousness is a disease that removes strength and veils intellect; insanity removes intellect and increases strength; intoxication is a lightness with visible effects on walk and speech. Key Terms
قهقهة (qahqaha): Laughing out loud in prayer, audible to both the person and his neighbors.
ضحك (dahik): Laughter audible only to the person himself; it invalidates prayer but not wudu.
تبسم (tabassum): Smiling with no audible sound; has no ruling.

Nullification of Wudu: Direct Touching
Intra-Hanafi dispute on touching nullifying wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nullification of wudu by direct touching of private parts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Direct touching of private parts nullifies wudu even without emission — the usual is treated as certain.

The text from Imdad al-Fattah explains a well-known point of ritual purity: when does touching private parts break wudu? Within the Hanafi school, there is a subtle difference between Abu Hanifa and his student Imam Muhammad. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to see how istihsan works alongside qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed act and ruling context
State the act in question: direct touching (mubashara fahisha) defined as touching the private part with an erect male organ without a barrier. Note that the ruling is whether this act nullifies wudu.
The text also mentions analogous cases: man-boy, two men, two women.
2
Analyze Imam Muhammad's qiyas argument
Imam Muhammad holds that wudu is nullified only if madhi (pre-ejaculate) is emitted, because emission can be verified directly (like other nullifiers). This is qiyas on the principle that nullifiers must be certain events.
Qiyas uses the certainty of emission as the effective cause (illa).
3
Evaluate Abu Hanifa's istihsan rebuttal
Abu Hanifa argues that touching usually leads to madhi, so the prevalent case (ghalib) is treated as certain (al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq). Rare cases without emission are ignored. This istihsan overrides strict qiyas.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) ينقض الوضوء المباشرة الفاحشة، وحقيقتها: (مس فَرْجِ بِذَكَرٍ مُنتصِب، بِلا حائل) يمنع وصــول حــرارة الجسد. واشتراط المس هو الظاهر، وقال بعضهم: لا يشترط المس. وقال محمد: لا ينقض الوضوء إلا بخروج مذي، وهو القياس، لأنه يمكن الوقوف على حقيقته، بخلاف التقاء الختانين، وجـه الاستحسان، أن المباشرة الفاحشة لا تخلو عن خروج مذي غالباً، والغالب كالمتحقق. ولا عبرة بالنادرة. وكذا المباشرة بين الرجل والغلام، وكذا بين الرجلين، والمرأتين.
(And) direct touching (mubashara fahisha) nullifies wudu, and its reality is: (touching the private part with a protruding male organ, without a barrier) that prevents the access of body heat. And the condition of touching is apparent, and some said: touching is not required. And Muhammad said: wudu is nullified only by the emission of madhi, and this is qiyas, because it can be verified, unlike the meeting of circumcised parts. The basis of istihsan is that direct touching usually does not lack emission of madhi, and the usual is like the certain. And the rare is not considered. And likewise, direct touching between a man and a boy, and likewise between two men, and two women.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses the nullification of wudu by direct touching (mubashara fahisha), defined as touching the private part (farj) with the male organ (dhakar) erect, without any barrier that prevents the feeling of body heat. The text notes that the condition of touching is the apparent view; some scholars say touching is not required—mere contact suffices. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that wudu is nullified only if madhi (pre-ejaculate) is emitted; this is the qiyas (analogical) position because emission is a verifiable event, unlike the mere meeting of circumcised parts (which is the condition for ghusl). The basis of istihsan (preferred, Abu Hanifa's view) is that such touching usually results in emission of madhi, and the prevalent case is treated as certain (al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq). Rare cases where no emission occurs are disregarded. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa: wudu is nullified by the act of touching even without emission. The ruling applies analogously to similar cases: touching between a man and a boy, between two men, and between two women.

Key Terms
المباشرة الفاحشة (al-mubashara al-fahisha): Direct touching of private parts without a barrier, specifically between male and female, or in analogous cases.
مس فرج بذكر منتصب (mass farj bi-dhakar muntasib): Touching the private part (farj) with a protruding (erect) male organ (dhakar).
مذي (madhi): Pre-ejaculate fluid that may be emitted during sexual arousal.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: Muhammad's method to deduce that wudu is nullified only by emission, by analogy with other nullifiers.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preference: the method used by Abu Hanifa to give the ruling based on the prevalent occurrence of madhi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), what nullifies wudu in cases of direct touching of private parts?
A) Only if madhi is emitted
B) The act of touching itself, even without emission
C) Only if the touching is intentional
D) Only if there is no barrier at all

Key Takeaway
Direct touching of private parts with an erect organ without barrier nullifies wudu immediately, per the relied-upon Hanafi position based on istihsan.
